RATING: 3
I knew this was like Age of Steam and I had to get my arm twisted to play it.
And I disliked it just like AoS.
Incredibly dry.  Zero fun. A brain-burner.
The best thing about the game was moving the train-shaped turn marker. Luckily I was sat at that corner of the board so I got to do that 6 times.
It took nearly 4 hours to play (5 players) but no-one seemed to be taking too long to take their turns.  A large amount of downtime, but arguably you need it to think about your next turn.
Life is too short to play games like this.
And this is when I won.

RATING: 3
An econ train game where the movement of goods functions in the polar opposite from how I see it logically playing out (where as you want to move them to the nearest port of business) in that you move goods the most inefficient chain possible to score the most points (compared to Indonesia where you pay per hop which makes sense). In that regard, my plays always felt sort of backwards in the analysis; I want track to go lots of places, but I don't want to move the goods there easily (but I do want to move the goods). An interesting puzzle, but not one that really holds my attention compared to other econ games. The digital version doesn't hold my attention after 9 or 10 plays, and plays in a fraction of the time as the cardboard copy...

RATING: 7
1 play / 5 players

LIKE: Good decisions on every turn. Role selection tied to turn order is interesting.
DISLIKE: Cutthroat and sometimes brutal, but not an unforgiving game. Poor planning can lead to a quick loss. 

OVERALL: I lost terribly by getting blocked and not having a backup plan. That aside, the game is tense and thoughtful. Plays relatively quick (2-3h?) for an involved train game. Something to play occasionally, but not an everyday game.

RATING: 9
Lo mejor de Wallace y uno de los mejores juegos de gestión y económicos que puedes jugar. Se trata de un simulador económico de trenes con muchísimas decisiones difíciles que tomar mientras construyes tu red, transportas mercancias, ayudas a desarrollar ciudades y pueblos, emites acciones y pagas a tus acreedores...
Se juega mejor que su predecesor, Age of Steam, y tiene componentes buenos y útiles (y aunque no trae los trenecitos de plástico de otros juegos eso no influye!). Además el juego viene con dos mapas y dos modos de juego, el básico y el estandard.
--------------------------------------------------
Best of Wallace's, and one of the best management or economic games you can play. Economic and train simulation with lots of hard choices to make: building up your railroad, delivering goods, developing cities and towns, issuing shares and paying back your creditors...
It has better gameplay than its predeccessor, Age of Steam, with nice and useful components (though it doesn't have those nice little plastic trains, this doesn't affect the gameplay). Also the game comes with two maps and two game modes, one basic and the other the standard one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Steam in its basic form is the excellent train game that combines all the good elements of Age of Steam and overcomes all the bits that don't work quite so well in Age of Steam. 

For example: the replenishment of resources to a city can now arrive in a package with some choices and not disrupt the game with luck; the turn order is predefined by previous choices rather than a bidding war; and you take loans out when you need then rather than planning for a potential eventuality. And all this works to streamline the game and make an already good game even more playable. Magnificent.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Age of Steam Redux.  Not having played the original AoS yet, I can't compare the two.  What I can say is that Steam plays quickly and smoothly.  I hear it's less painful and more forgiving than AoS, not to mention faster.

I enjoyed it and would gladly play again.  An excellent train game.  By all means, give Steam a shot.

EDIT:  Years later and with another play under my belt, I can see the correlations in Steam with Railways of the World and, to a much lesser extent, with 18xx games.  Steam is very much a euro-styled implementation of the Railways games (which are introductory towards 18xx).

My biggest issue with Steam is how I tend to run low on deliverables at the end of the game.  It's not super critical, but it's tight enough that I dislike the squeeze.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First Review February 2012:  Steam is probably the best "train game" that I have ever played, though in truth, I am not that big a fan of "train games." As such, my best "train game" merits a maximum rating of...8.

Second Review May 2012:  I find that I prefer Railroad Tycoon to Steam because it leavens the dominant, overbearing concern with delivering cubes to "get those victory points" with some additional, interesting (dare I say fun?) ways to earn victory points in other ways, namely, linking cities and satisfying secret goals (ala Ticket To Ride), delivery bonuses, and hotels.  This ever so slight leavening of the one dimensional "must deliver cubes" approach I think improves the gaming experience.  In particular, RR Tycoon provides evident, additional paths to victory, and that is almost always a good thing in a game. So...Steam drops to a 7, and RR Tycoon earns the 8.

RATING: 9
A fun and streamlined railroad game.  Features some really intuitive mechanics which aids new players in focusing more about game decisions and less on rules.  The components all work well, and while the ownership markers may be more boring compared to Age of Steam or Railways of the world, the track tiles seem to be of much better quality and of greater variety.  Other aspects I appreciate in steam is the improved Goods cube allocation, in that City Growth actions and Urbanization can be better planned for.  Overall the well designed components and streamlined nature of Steam make it both a very fun and Strategic experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game, while very good, is no Age of Steam (which I rate a 10).  The reason: the lack of goods production chart.  Without any randomness regarding when goods will come out, you don't need to be as flexible, and without the look-ahead provided by the chart the game is significantly less strategic.  What I mean by this is that the game loses almost all of the pre-game planning found in Age of Steam, where you look at the board and the goods display to try to come up with a plan: where are my 2-deliveries, my 3's, etc.  All the way up to trying to find the cubes you (hope to) be delivering at the end of the game.  Of course, you can't assume the other players won't interfere: it's usually best to have several different plans in mind at this state.  In Steam the altered production mechanic makes it much easier to have the cubes you need when you need them, so this planning is greatly reduced.  Unfortunately, this planning is the strongest part of the game.

I'm not trying to get into the war over which of these is superior and whether John Bohrer or Martin Wallace bakes better cookies.  I feel that the best part of Age of Steam has been developed away in this game.  The remaining game is good, even very good, but it is not excellent.  It is, however, much more accessible for this reason.

The strategy is very high and rich, and the player interaction medium. I like this game. I would play it if someone put it on the table.  The problem is that I don't like the mechanic very much: pushing cubes around routes isn't my favorite. There is one overarching strategy: (1) Build a continuous route closest to the most goods, so that you can transport your goods, and force your opponents to transport their goods over your routes (while minimizing the amount that you have to transport on their routes). Nonetheless, out of this strategy births a lot of interesting strategy and game play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Steam is a refined version of its brethren: Age of Steam and Railways of the World.
The basic game is akin to RotW, with a tighter map and income, and without the randomness of the cards.
The standard game is a beast similar to Age of Steam, with some of the rough edges smoothed. The duality about the income or points is perhaps cleaner than in AoS, but I'm not a fan. Some other aspects, like the City Growth action, are actually better in Steam.
Overall, it's one of the best pick up and deliver train games out there.

RATING: 8.8
Comments are for the base game. Steam has instantly become my favorite train game. That's not saying very much as I generally don't like these games very much. But this must be the exception to the rule. This is the cleanest and most crisp Wallace design I ever played. I like the way income and trains are simplified, I like the fact you have more influence on the goods added to the board, I like the role selection and removal of auctions. This game flows like a charm. It is very deep and challenging without becoming convoluted. This is an amazing design I recommend to anyone looking for a relatively quick but challenging pick-up and delivery game.

RATING: 9
Great game whether you use base or standard rules. It offers a lot of replayability. Build your track wisely so you can eventually move goods over 5 or 6 links and be aware of where other player build their network. Play also very well with 2 players on smaller maps (such as Sardinia, Scotland, Sicily and Malta, etc.) or expansion #1. My only complaint would be related to the last few turns when you struggle to ship the scarce resources and to expand your network (not much room left for railroad building).

RATING: 10
Lovingly crafted variation on Age of Steam by Martin Wallace via Mayfair. Most everything that you liked about AoS can be here for you, along with modifications for play that are more forgiving and streamlined as introductory options.

The basic game is for new players and is very forgiving while introducing people to the general system. The built in turn order associated with the roles works but may frustrate more experienced players who will soon want to opt for the auctions to more properly account for the changing importance of the roles as the game progresses.

The dice-driven city growth scheme, and the blind cube-grab Production role, have been replaced by a supply track that allows players more control over where cubes appear whether by Urbanization or Goods Production (a role that now is one of the more desirable choices for role selection). The income track is now split between income or VPs with the player having the choice which to choose when completing a delivery (income levels at game end are converted to VPs but at a discount). At first blush these changes correct most of what people found problematic with AoS; however, the end-game rush to fill in track links to burn up remaining money and squeeze out a few extra VPs still remains.

Component quality and map design is excellent and the map is hard mounted and reversible (US/Germany). The track tiles are sturdy and slightly thicker than those in AoS and the selection of complex track types more diverse. The need to use grey disks to indicate town-sites under just laid track hexes, which some AoS players found confusing, has been eliminated here as all combinations of track through town-sites have their own track tiles now.

All-in-all, an excellent edition of this enduring game - one of Martin's best has become even better!
++++++
I have stopped thinking of the Base Game as "introductory" and the Standard Game as "advanced." Both versions of the game are quite complete in their own right. I think it is a further strength of the game that it gives you two perfectly legitimate ways to play.

RATING: 9.1
I find Steam to be a cut and dried version of a train game.

RATING: 9
Heresy, I know, for those who know and love AoS; but this is a better game!  I like AoS a lot but that was despite its minor flaws rather than because of them.  

For my money, Steam fixes the two main issues with AoS - that is the loan/interest and replacement goods/cubes sub-systems.  Both are just a bit footery in AoS (particularly the cube system, which is a bit too random, especially in the context of the rest of the game - it just seemed a bit out of place; 'tacked on' even).  Steam smoothes out both these minor annoyances and integrates the sub-systems much better with the rest of the game - which also speeds up gameplay - bonus!  Another side benefit of the fix for interest is a new balance to be struck between VPs and income, which adds a nice little extra.


The rest is the same glorious game as AoS, though with better production values and a double sided map.

This shows the benefit of some extra development by a designer who now has much more experience than when AoS first came out.  I know there will be some who will prefer the way things were but I'm in the 'improved' camp - which surprises me, as I rated AoS so highly I doubted it could be much improved.  Glad to be wrong on that.

P.S. A final heresey - whisper it softly, but the 'no turn auction' basic game works well and speeds things up enough to make me seriously consider whether it might just be better to always play that way!  A few plays confirms that it works well and I really doubt whether the extra time and angst added by the full 'standard' rules are worth it most of the time - so, go ahead, mark me down under the 'wimp' column - but only so long as you tick the 'masochist' box if you disagree!


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Played once, I had good expectations that with Mayfair Martin Wallace might *finally* get an experienced and competent developer and turn out a decent game, and first impressions are that this expectation has been met. A lot of the ridiculously silly, anti-intuitive wonkiness of Age of Steam is gone, the rough edges filed off, some semblance of balance attained, and things generally cleaned up quite a bit. Also, the two included maps seem very solid and interesting, where the Age of Steam maps were hit and miss, and the "rust belt" one in the base game was actually one of the weaker ones. For me, the "refined" "Base" game is definitely where it's at, and I think it will find more fans than the "Standard" game, which is basically just slightly tweaked Age of Steam. While the income/VP dichotomy has helped clean up things in a lot of ways (even if it makes no thematic sense), the turn-order auction that is the core difference between "Base" and "Standard" is still hugely problematic in Standard.

Of the three attempts at this so far (Age of Steam, Railroad Tycoon, and now Steam), this is the only one where you can't hear the gears grinding and tearing in the background. It's still a tough, analytical game that sometimes seems to have been designed to make you feel like an idiot, but it's nowhere near as gratuitously hard as Age of Steam or crazily unplaytested as Railroad Tycoon, and first impressions are that it plays rather well.

RATING: 10
Steam is a game that has been in my collection for quite some time and is one of the first "serious" games I purchased. I tend to sell off or trade games that lose their luster over time to keep my collection at a manageable level, and despite that, Steam has remained a permanent fixture in my collection.

What is the draw? Steam has incredibly tight mechanics that are relatively easy to explain but generate some tough decisions. I tend to like games that add a spatial element, and the pickup and delivery system of Steam does exactly that while evoking a theme. 

The way I play Steam has also evolved as I've evolved as a gamer. My first few plays were of the base ruleset. Then I tried the standard (and tougher) rules with "serious" gamers, and it opened up my enjoyment of the game even further. I've gone on to acquire all the various map expansions, which then opened up the flexibility of the core rules and an appreciation for Steam as a game system. 

The result is a game that:
* Can be taught to people relatively new to the hobby, or seasoned vets.
* Can play 2 to 6 with perfect scalability.
* Can offer a fresh, new map experience to pretty much anyone.

Basically I'm "all in" at this point and I hope I can continue to convince other gamers to explore all these various maps with me, as I don't expect my joy of this game to wane anytime soon.

RATING: 9
I'm a long fan of Age of Steam.

I have to admit, I think on the whole I like Steam at least as much. I think the production action is MUCH stronger, and makes the game more strategic in some ways. Its an interesting new lever to play with. I need more plays to judge better.

The changed scoring is a neutral to me. 

The production values are very nice, however I always did like the stark simplicity of the Warfrog version of Age of Steam. 

One thing I really don't like...the bizarre distribution of the double-sided tiles. Its just about impossible to know exactly what is available at any given time. There was an avenue of strategy where you could limit an opponent's builds by consuming the special tiles they needed. Now...it feels very vague and haphazard. I'd prefer a detailed manifest and single-sided tiles (yes, I know they'd need more).  Perhaps just make all the straight / gentle curves double sided...but the special tiles should not be.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Steam is a redevelopment of the AoS system. For the most part, the game retains the feel and options of the original, though there are marked differences, which I will spend most of my time commenting on. Up front, I want to say that this is a distinct variation of the gaming system, and that it is best bought with the intent of simply playing Steam, the same way AoS is bought best for playing AoS. Both of the games can be cobbled into the other, but neither will play optimally when they're being used in that way. The biggest changes here are the tile mix (sometimes this is important, sometimes not; definitely much moreso if using the designer and publisher's preferred rules where the tile mix is unlimited here), the production system, and the split income tracks. Of these, the split tracks are the thing that I think will most divide the system because they fundamentally change the economy of the game.

The beginner rules are, frankly, a bit boring for anyone who is used to the original system. I could not see using most of the rules implemented in it beyond the initial learning game or two though they are still markedly better than the other easier form of the system that is available in Railroad Tycoon. The auction replacement scheme I do like though. It is interesting, but is not well-suited to base-game system + expansion use as it forces the designer to numerically define the role values for the players, which takes away some of the fun of learning new boards. I do think the idea is a good one for an off-board or two, and I would like to see it used on an expansion or two with a more traditional, cut-throat economy as I like the trade-offs involved in choosing power roles. I'm glad they decided to ditch this from the full game though, as novel as it is, I think the auction is a much better role choosing mechanic in general for a large variety of reasons.

The production scheme replacement in general is too powerful and easy to manipulate in the early game , but I can't fault them on that as fixing growth is difficult and I don't think anyone has gotten the right mix between restricted and laissez faire yet. The Steam system is a cleaner, more controllable way to conduct growth, albeit with a cost, that cost being another really good role in the auction, as it jacks the value of the role in the early going and can make early profitability happen pretty quickly if you are isolated in any given location on the board. The downside of the dice is their random nature, you can be screwed for the long game by them if your luck takes a turn for the worse. The downside of this is that it is easier to set up optimum runs, especially in the early game when you need them most. A key element of the goods growth scheme in the original game is that it ties goods to certain locations on the board, you can't manipulate that. Here you can, and as a result, cubes are much more likely to end up in places you want them rather than in places where you have to figure out how to use them (with the latter making for more interesting decisions/challenges in practice). Production is easily on par with Urbanization in the first couple of rounds, if not better in some situations. If you're isolated, it's a possible #1 pick as it makes it very easy to get dream setups in the early going that allow double 3 ships on round 2 to blast up the income track and get the ball rolling. I think with some restrictions, this idea is a fundamentally more sound concept, though definitely best at either higher scales or with at least one of the traditional good roles neutered. The main thing I would look at doing is   tying the cubes to given locations to keep Production from being too powerful and the cubes from being too controllable. The power of production here is high enough to basically make it a rough equal with Urb and those two roles only a half step down from Locomotive in value. What this means is that there is almost no incentive to bid in the auction unless you intend to win Loco, and being in 2nd-3rd is about equal in value until the production cubes run out.

The other major change is the split income and VP tracks. I can see where people might enjoy the gamey nature of this more than the gamey nature of the original (i.e.: if you have a big engine you constantly have to take out shares to build track because income maxes at 10) but my main concern is simply that it makes reduction harder to implement, and I'm extremely fond of that rule because of the little screw-you situations that it creates. I think it also is effectively trying to do the same thing (slowing down leaders, and the person with the biggest engine should be the leader in this game), but it strikes me as even gamier than the original system. This doesn't bother me in either case because I'm more interested in the decisions being created than any sort of thematic integrity, but there you are. This scheme also seems to make the money much looser throughout the game as the tendency is to zero out income (and have a huge pile of cash laying around) so that any ship for income is just back up to cover Loco expenses or to max out the track at 10. This feels extremely loose compared to the AoS system. This splitting of the tracks was touted as adding a decision point to the game, but I don't feel that it does. You need money to operate, so early ships are going to be for income. When you get up to 10, if you still anticipate needing liquid capital, you cash out at the beginning of the round and then ship yourself back up to 10. There's not a lot of thought that goes into it. It has the side effect of creating the aforementioned piles of cash which means it is harder to really put the thumbscrews to someone with a well-placed piece of track or a carefully thought out bid, leeching tension from the game and weapons away from the players.

As a real aside, a lot of the niggly, extra rules that have been added to this are irksome. i.e.: artificially forcing players to deliver cubes using at least half of their own links, not allowing people with engines of 6 to take Locomotive, and the like. These rules both take away occasionally useful options and crowd the rulebook with extra rules that flat out aren't needed and add extra learning overhead to the game.

As most people have seen now, this game is fairly close to AoS. The changes that are present are significant though. Production is more powerful and controllable, which makes setting up desirable shipments easier. The split tracks tend to soften the economy, making it harder to really hurt someone with a well-placed piece of track or bid in the auction.

As an overall base system, this is similar to Age of Steam, but it seems to be better tuned for higher player count games out of the box because of the role power enhancement. Dedicated expansions should be able to easily fix this though, just like they did for AoS. The overall cube tightness is laudable in terms of left over pieces, the rest of the game feels loose to  me, but then again, I'm not the target audience so what I have to say is largely immaterial. Essentially, if you have quibbles with the aspects of Age of Steam that are addressed here you'll probably be very happy with the game and enjoy it a good deal more than AoS. People who are already converted fans of the original game are less likely to find a lot to like here, because most of the changes seem to be aimed at making the game easier to play out of the box, which is likely to have little resonance with people who are already familiar with the game.

Rating is based on my expansion ranking system with 5 being the base and the rank being moved up or down based on that. Pretty much the only thing I like here is the way the cubes run out and the base, though not the final implementation of the production system. Other than that, I think the changes take away interesting weapons or player choices that were present in the original game. If this was an AoS expansion, it would be one of the weaker ones available because I think it takes the game backwards from where I want it to be.

RATING: 7
It's really all about the never-ending discussion: [i]Which is better, Steam or Age of Steam?[/i] The answer: [i]It depends on what you find more important[/i]. And for me, the specifics put Steam ahead of its older sibling Age of Steam.

First, Steam's way of splitting up loans (shares/stock/...) and points leads to gameplay which simply gets does not get in the way of the player as much as does Age of Steam's, which multiplexes points, income and income reduction into a single track and keeps shares as a simple counter. I find this gives people a much better idea of where they are in the game. Second, Steam's method of handling production and resource replenishment is much more acceptable to me. In Age of Steam a shortage of goods can result in brutal games, but really, what is the point of such brutality if the players are at the mercy of unmitigated dice? I'd rather trade in the wide, epic and uncontrollable scope, for a more limited but controllable, and still difficult to manage deterministic system. And alter rules elsewhere if nastiness is desired. These two points seem minor, but they mean a great deal to me, causing me to give the nod to Steam instead of Age of Steam.

I also like how the rules to Steam incorporate two variants: one for more casual play, and one which is very close to Age of Steam. If it no big deal to backport those simpler rules to Age of Steam, but still, the fact that this variant is in the rule book and not on some weird fan website is a pleasant idea. 

The games themselves play nearly identically as far as I could tell, so there is little to mention in terms of extras here. I have given Steam the original rate I gave Age of Steam (a 7), and downgraded the original's by 1 point to highlight that an in my opinion superior system exists. I might even decide to swap my copy of Age of Steam for a copy of Steam.

RATING: 7
Through a quirk of circumstance, I first played this exactly three days after first playing Age of Steam.  We played the Base Game, and the difference was ridiculous.  I'd rate Base Steam a 3 or 4; I simply have no interest in playing it again.  I understand the impulse, to draw people put off by the otherwise very difficult and inaccessible Age of Steam, but (1) I enjoy that kind of difficulty, and (2) from what I can tell, people that play and like Base Steam have no interest in progressing to Standard Steam or Age of Steam.  So what's the point?  Play what is good and what you enjoy, not some intro/lite version of it.

I somewhat regret getting this instead of the Age of Steam reprint.  At least there is this: most of my gaming opponents have copies of Age of Steam so if I want that experience I can have it.  Standard Steam seems, most generously, to be a streamlining of Age of Steam, and I'm hopeful that to a new initiate the differences will be minor.  But there is little to recommend it above and beyond the earlier game.  At best it is an equal variant.

RATING: 6
That delicious moment when you can stop focusing on income and start churning out victory points: that's what this game is all about.  Building inefficient routes to cities so that later on you can move goods as far as possible in order to collect the most points possible.  While you're at it, cut your competitors off by building routes through a path that they intended to use.  Better yet, just steal a good that they were setting up a route to earn 6 points with.  Sure, you might only get 2 points, but their disapproving stare is more than worth it!

There's a lot going on here, and it is important to not allow one player to have tons of space to operate uninterrupted.  If someone has a section of the board where they are the lone rail builders, they will have a HUGE advantage over the other players.

Don't shy away from conflict in this game.  Enjoy the moments of dickery and understand and accept the fact that what goes around comes around.

RATING: 8.5
Streamlined Age of Steam with more tough decisions because of the split Income/VP track.  Being able to use an entire delivery to increase the income makes it less brutal, but it works well.  Map looks nice, great bits, and I like the new City Growth rules that replace the randomness of the former Production action in AoS.  I still like AoS, but I definitely I prefer Steam.  The Standard version is best, but I like that the Base game is an option if I want to teach newbies or want to get in a quicker game.  

To me, it's more strategic than AoS, because I don't have to worry so much about bankruptcy in the beginning, and I can focus on long-term shipping possibilities, rather than "where am I going to find something to ship *this turn* in order to keep from losing VPs?" I can employ a much more aggressive strategy in Steam than was possible in AoS. Steam eliminates the randomness and some of the brutality (not all!) to make for an overall more enjoyable gaming experience.

RATING: 7
[September 2017 - 7]
Steam gets a bump up thanks to the mobile app version of the game, which gets rid of all the tile-laying fiddliness and helps move the money/loans/interest calculations along faster.

I still find the "inefficient" deliveries annoying with respect to the theme, but it makes for a unique game/puzzle with good interaction.

[May 2015 - 6]
I'm surprised how much I didn't like this game, as I'm a Martin Wallace fan and it receives generally good praise as one of the better pickup-and-deliver games.

First, what I like ...

I like the board, the income mechanism and choice for victory points. Although the $5 for $1 (on debt), then $1 for $1 on interst/income can feel disjointed. And I feel as if income/money should count for a few more victory points at the end.

I like all the decisions and I see the strategies, especially when it comes to placing and playing cubes in vicinity to your opponents (Do I deliver this color good to get the most points, or this color good for fewer points, but my opponent will get fewer as well?) As many Euros, it's a good balancing act, and surprisingly, there's some nice interaction, provided your tracks are intersecting. The randomness of the cubes that come out and where they are placed lend to some nice replayability.


What I don't like ...

The tile-laying is fiddly with the double-sided tiles and having to hunt for the right one. It slows the game down.

Half of the roles seem kinda weak

My biggest issue is the theme within the goods delivery, which I can best describe as 'disconnected.' Some might say 'abstract' is a better word, but it's not that ... I see cities, tracks and terrain, so the visual cues of nice train game are there, but something doesn't quite flow.

With goods delivery, you're rewarded for the amount of links you have on your track (cities and towns along the way). At the same time, you're not rewarded for being efficient with your deliveries in Steam. You take the longer route with more links between two cities to score more points (even if you own a shorter connection).

This would make sense if you were carrying several different goods and dropping them off along the way for points, but you aren't. You can only deliver one cube. There's no penalty for taking that longer route with more cities, other than perhaps the initial locomotive investment.

Meanwhile, a long uninterrupted route (without cities in-between) forms just one "link" -- say four tiles between two cities -- and gets paid out $1, the same as one uninterrupted tile between two cities.

That disjointedness rattles the brain a bit. Again, it's not abstract -- I can do abstract. Steam, rather, sets you up with the theme and then throws you for a loop -- literally, loops and zig-zag track lines can benefit you in this game.

I see why some of it is the way it is, I just wish they would have been able to tie it all together a little better with the theme. Maybe I just don't like pick-up-and-deliver games, because I've tried a few and don't really enjoy them. (Any better suggestions?)

I found myself wanting to play Puerto Rico, Power Grid, Airlines Europe or Suburbia instead of Steam (for similar mechanics).

I will say, that once you know what you're doing and you can get past the tile hunting, the game moves a bit quicker than those above, but I can totally see people getting bogged down in the decisions.

[2009-12-07 Rating: n.a. -> 8]
Just a few comments after my first game (with basic rules):

* The basic game of Steam plays like Railroad Tycoon (RRT) and Rails of Europe (RoE) with just a few minor twists (like role-selection). As I like these two quite a lot, I naturally liked Steam. On the other hand, there weren't enough differences to really justify the purchase of Steam. So, until I play this one with the full set of rules and see whether it improves the game and sets it apart from RRT and RoE, I won't add it to my collection.

* The Steam map is better than the map in Railroad Tycoon and on par with the one of Rails of Europe.

* There isn't so much chrome component-wise as you would get with Railroad Tycoon or Rails of Europe. Everything is "just" functional.

RATING: 8
This game is so close to Age of Steam, it should have just been released as an expansion map to Age of Steam (incorporating different rules).  If it did, it would be considered "friendly/family" rules.  The economic system is much more forgiving. It takes less future planning.  The tiles for railbuilding are friendlier.  The angst of the auction has been removed.

All of these make Age of Steam better in my opinion.  But although I like the original more, this isn't bad by any stretch.  There is actually one rule in Steam that I like better...the production action.  It's almost irrelevant in Age of Steam, but works better in Steam.

One thing that I really did not care for in Steam was the fact that "Income Reduction" is not present in any form.  This is a good "catch-up" mechanism in AoS and represents the theme well.  Steam feels very linear in terms of VP point growth.  Once you get the lead, it felt like it was easier to play "tit for tat" and keep others from gaining on you.

Age of Steam = 10   (many plays on various maps)
Steam = 7  (1 4er game, 2 5er game new rules)

Update: Recently played this with the Age of Steam auction rules.  The only noticeable differences are: no dice for production, production gets 3 cubes to choice of city, loans available during mid-turn at a reduced rate, no income reduction.   That said, this ruleset (I can't remember the name) is much better than what I had played before.  I'd rate this a 9...almost as good as AoS (a 10).  I think this game thus settles into an average of 8.

RATING: 6
Players manage their income to develop and run a railway.  Players take turns to jockey for turn order, tighten their finances for the turn, then build track over either a map of the Ruhr or Canada/NE.USA.  Goods are then run over the built track to generate income or victory points.  Most points after a set number of turns wins.  Tough game, latest in a long series that began 12 years ago, but honed to perfection here.  Two ways to play means you can tune your session to either fast or full game, and both work very well.  In fact, I think the "Base" game is better than the more complex "Standard" game, but at least you get the choice.  All the previous games had their issues - crappy Winsome joke DTP components of the early railways game; Volldampf... actually, Volldampf is pretty good; Age of Steam with it's warts and baggage; the strange Railway Tycoon with the huge map and weird track set.  Probably as good as it's going to get.

Update: Megkapta a 9-est. És talán picit jobb is. Lehet, h az is segített ebben, h játszottam egyet a Baron kieggel is, de így az újabb Steam játékban már nem kísértett a RT, egyszerűen csak a játék volt. Kicsit el tud húzódni, és vannak részek amikor gyorsabb gondolkodásúaknak is le kell állni molyolni, de a fázisok nagy része pörgősen megy. Ugyanakkor mély analízis van benne, komoly stratégia, és az ellenfelek játékát is igen lehet -sőt kell- befolyásolni.

RATING: 4
Steam, while mechanically sound, fails to maintain thematic continuity and becomes disjointed. What begins as a gratifying race for key routes and city access devolves into a competition for creating the most convoluted delivery routes. The game rewards players for delivering through as many towns as possible, which is antithetical to how a delivery corps should function. In reality short and quick deliveries are preferred, but in Steam players must create an increasingly serpentine web of rail lines. This inconsistency makes the game frustratingly counterintuitive and produces a strong enough disconnect between the theme and mechanics, that makes even the most mechanics-first gamers cringe.

RATING: 6

The game is so forgiving and non-variable that the player who jumped out to a lead invariably would grind it out to victory. I now appreciate Age of Steam’s punishing income and loan system as well as the risk management required due to the random nature of cube placement.

RATING: 6
Not my sort of game. If you already like Martin Wallace games, heavier games, train games, and similar build-track-deliver-cubes games, you will love this. The balance between earning money or Victory Points is nicely handled, the basic decisions are easy enough, but with the timing, and only really being able to plan 1 turn ahead (because of the changing track layouts) makes this a slow game. 

I have a major grudge too. The basic track piece where a straight track splits off to a separate line is NOT IN THIS GAME. So in a very stupid way, you have to build parallel track to track you've already built. This may suit the game mechanics itself (you get a VP for each new line you build), but seems very unnatural. It makes joining up different parts of your network near impossible.

Anyway, if you like this sort of thing, you'll love Steam. If you don't, you won't.

RATING: 8.5
This game has a great deal in common with Railroad Tycoon. It's a very demanding game, and very tight. It adds some elements, around the way you upgrade engines, cities, and select turn order. It takes away the "reward cards" or whatever they're called in RRT. I'd say that I like each game almost the same. I like the game play in this one better, I like the epic scope of Railroad Tycoon. Since I love epic games, RRT gets the slight nod. Still, after only one play, it's a much loved train game.

EDIT: OK, after my second play, I see the very significant differences with RRT. I'll say I still like RRT more, but this one is lots of fun. Harder in lots of ways. Theres a very interesting mechanic where you take your increase for delivering goods either in income or VP. That's a very notable difference and makes the game, in my mind, more of a mental challenge.

RATING: 9.5
This is one of my all-time favorite games. Normally I play the called  "standard game" 'cause feel it more tight, tense and competitive; even though, I also enjoy the "basic game" 'cause it's more "friendly" but still has lots of strategy involved. 

I know there are many fans of Age of Steam, the "older brother" of Steam and I'm pretty sure that game must be incredibly good, but to me, Steam has all I have always wanted from a train themed game: lots of player interaction, extremely low luck factor, a system that allows different play-styles and rewards strategical thought, great components, etc. 

I own the map expansion #2. Which includes: 1st. China (a 2-3 players map and I love it for allowing an excellent Steam experience for 2 players); 2nd. California (a 3 players map that can be played like 2 different scenarios, one in present time and the other in a futuristic timeline); and 3rd. Great Britain (a brilliant expansion for 3-4 players that emulates the use of canals in the GB of the industrial revolution. My favorite Steam expansion to date.).  

What else can I say?... Spectacular! A must-have one!

RATING: 8
A reworking and streamlining of an earlier Martin Wallace game. That does conjure up some bad memories. Unfortunately, they turn out to apply here as well. Fortunately, they do so to a much lower degree.

Much of the basic system remains unchanged. The major differences to AoS are the loan and income system which is much less brutal and therefore supposedly more suitable for new players, and a split between income for the companies and victory points for the players. Both of these don't seem much like improvements to me.

The game did improve on a somewhat smaller scale, though. Production now allows the player to choose from a limited selection of cubes instead of randomly drawing from the bag, and the costs for laying track make a little more sense, too.

Overall, Steam is no AoS. It is, however, still a very good game.

Steam takes a game that was truly unique among the pantheon of Eurogames and turns it into just another neutered, tactical waste of tablespace.  Burn it.

RATING: 9
Great economic game with a load of interaction - building tracks, sending cubes, bidding for position and choosing special tiles. I prefer it much more than RRT - it's heavier, tighter, deeper, with shorter railway links and without overproduced componets. Steam is much better produced than AoS - colors match (grey cubes go to grey cities), components are sturdy and good-looking, there are cardboard coins - much better than paper money (because money is not secret). I also like the changed production - production table in AoS is a worse solution IMO, because if you're unlucky some cubes will never be put on cities or will be put too late. I prefer to fight for putting cubes I want on cities I want. Spliting VP and income tracks in Steam also works good. To recap - I want to play more. One more thing - I can also play AoS. Both games are really good. I just find Steam better.

RATING: 7.5
Wallace revision of classic AoS - played 1st solo game, basic version, and took half the game getting into the black.  This is difficult, even on the 'easy' setting, with as many choices as a game like Brass, but with significantly tougher access to profitability.

I waited a long time getting a VP engine going while I generated income potential 1st play, and in hindsight that might have been overly cautious (and more appropriate in the full 'Standard' game with AoS-like rules).

Rated this a 7 on 1st solo play, with a lot of potential upside based on additional play.  It may be real difficult selling this to the family though- this is a real gamers' game, with a lot of reward coming in the play, and not much mercy granted.

After playing with the 'Standard' game and also now having playing AoS (1x) and Railroad Tycoon (1x), I think I like Steam best of the three.

The greater control of Goods Growth appeals, as does the nicer artwork, with the simplicity of AoS, but a nicer look (like RT, but without the plastic bits).  Raised to a 7.5.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After a disastrous introduction to Age of Steam, and being spoiled by Railroad Tycoon, I was hesitant to try Steam. It turns out that I like the "basic" version of Steam, without the maintenance costs and the flexibility to take loans at any time during your turn. The "standard" rules from what I hear are pretty much AOS--locomotive maintenance and loans only at the beginning of your turn. The basic rules do away with bidding for player order, and replace it with a cleaner, more streamlined mechanism.

I cannot reconcile the fact that I vehemently hate AOS, and the feeling it invoked when I played it. the standard rules for Steam seem to be the same. I don't think I'll care to play that version, but will insist on the "basic" rules.

As for the components, Steam is a massive improvement over the 2nd AOS (from what I could see in the photos), and still much better than the 3rd edition. The plastic trains in the 3rd edition just remind me that they were ripped off from Railroad Tycoon, where they are more at home. The graphics are gorgeous, and the wooden disks pay homage to the 2nd edition of AOS, although the board gets cluttered quickly with many colored wooden disks--not quite the impact of a developed RRT board.

The play is tighter than RRT, and the map is smaller, making for very cramped, if not cut-throat play. The rules for track-laying is cleaner than AOS, but not as simple as RRT. The Urbanization and City Growth options are straight-forward, instead of the convoluted, unpredictable setup in AOS. Otherwise, the options are the same between Steam and AOS. Also, there is no bidding for player order in the basic rules--order is determined by what action you picked the previous turn. This seems cleaner and faster than the clunky bidding in AOS.

All in all, I think playing AOS prepared me for Steam. I like the basic rules much better than AOS, and if I ever want to return to that hell that is AOS, I can switch over to "standard" rules and proceed to slash my wrists. It definitely scratches the AOS itch, if I ever had one, as Steam is more intense than RRT. The play is more fluid than AOS, but not as much as RRT. So, I'd say this is a happy medium between the two extremes. At least I don't need a large table to play this!

Update (01/22/12): Initial rating was an '8'. However, compared to RRT/RotW, I'd rather play those (and I own RRT). Do I want to own this? No. Do I have a desire to play this again? No. Downing to a '7'.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Age of Steam with John Bohrer's contribution taken out, and very much the better for it. There's no random die rolling for goods growth, no dreary Rustbelt map. Strangely, the Basic Game is the better of the two rulesets with its rotating action selection mechanism a vast improvement on the ghastly auction for turn order in the Standard Game (at least if you want to go Standard there are now three strong action choices: locomotive, production and urbanize). 
In spite of its obvious cleverness, this is possibly the game that I most love to hate. It's all about minimaxing which isn't something I enjoy, nor something I'm  particularly good at. You can be quite arbitrarily screwed over by another player during building and shipping. Playing at low scale (2-3 player) avoids some of this, but 18xx is a vastly better train game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First played September 30th, 2009. Taught in 20 min, played in 2.5 hours for 3 players. This game has pretty straitforward strategy and good strategy/luck balance. The object is to gain the most points by building you train empire in 10 rounds. The start player gets $0 and each following player gets $1 to begin. Each player has one of 7 options each round. The start player picks first, followed by the order on the "turn order track". 1) Be the start player, 2) Move goods first, 3) First build. 4) Lay 4 track instead of one, 5) City Growth (cost $2), 6) Add one locomotive (cost $4+$1*locomative total moving to), 7) Add new city (cost $6). Next players build 3 track (unless you get 4) starting with the player that took the build first action followed by "turn order" (tracks must connect to city or another track piece. If the track is not finished on the next round, it is removed). Next each player may take two move actions(move train down; or move a good between two cities gaining $1/city on the income track or 1unit/city moved on VP track; cubes are moved from one city to another until they reach the matching color of themselves; can only move one city per train track level you are currently at; if you move across an opponents track, they recieve the point). After movement, each player collects $ designated on the $ track. If it's negative, they pay that amount. Next turn order is reset based on lowest value first.

RATING: 8.5
July09:
That's a darn good game boys and girls.  I really appreciate the compact presentation.  It's just a board and some tiles and a few extra bits.  Nothing like the fiasco that is Railroad Tycoon.

I've only played the Basic game, but I'd imagine that's all I'd play.  The decisions come fast and furious.  And it is fun to have your own little railroad barony by the end of the game.

As for specifics:

I do like action selection -> turn order.

Split Income/VP is just silly.  I'm thinking about bringing the AoS system back just for this part.

Double sided track tiles are SWEET.

Production is smooth a silk.  I thought it might ruin long term planning, but that's just silly.  With 12 options, the worst that can happen is you don't get your first pick.  The marginal value of the second choice is still pretty damn solid.

If you're a train fan, this is the one to grab.

Now I just have to offload my RRT for a chunk of change.

Aug17: Still solid.  Usually play a hodge-podge of Steam/AoS rules.

RATING: 7
Great graphics. Solid take on the AoS/RRT type of game, with the Base game occupying a somewhat successful middle ground between the extremes represented by those two other games. The Standard game is *very* close to AoS.

I am not really a fan of the new concept of differentiating between income and points, and feel that both the AoS and RRT family of games have better, more intuitive economic models. That being said, Base game Steam is more accessible and approachable than AoS. Personally, I suspect that at the end of the day I prefer the added complexity and playing time of AoS, 'cause the auctions at the beginning of each turn really add to the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
After half a dozen plays, I still think this is a great game. The auctions in the standard game create a nice self-balancing mechanism for the value of the various actions, and there are lots of significant decisions to be made. The game starts to shine when you know it well enough to plan your moves several turns in advance, and it's great when you can do the same thing for your opponents moves and see key opportunities to block them.

My only complaint is that the default map is a bit too open for three players. In my experience, this means that the map ends up roughly divided into thirds, and the player in the middle is the only person interacting with the other two players. That player can balance the game by making sure neither of the other players runs away with the game, but a couple of turns with less than maximum efficiency can make it hard for them to win. (This might be recency bias, though, since that's what happened in my most recent game. It was still close, though: there were only three points between first and third place.)

2013-12-13

Initial Rating: 7.0 (November 2010)

What a great game! I have played it long ago and I remember I did like the game, but I feel I was too new to the hobby to really appreciate this gem. I played it again yesterday and it was really great!

It is a very good meaty game, with low (zero?) luck after the setup. You have to carefully manage your debt (VPs vs. income, really interesting decisions) and really plan ahead. 

I don’t particularly like the trains theme, but it fits perfectly with the game mechanics, perhaps the game can take a long time to play with 4 or 5 players. The game components are gorgeous, way ahead of my copy of AoS.

I really want to play AoS again soon, I played it long ago and don’t remember the rules and differences. I know Steam is more forgiving, so I want to play the hardest game :D. I’m upgrading Steam rating to a 8.0. A very good game I will always play. When I play again AoS I will review this rating.

I have played a lot of AoS recently. I don’t particularly subscribe to the philosophy that a game replaces another, but in this case AoS replaces Steam for me. RATING: 10
Strategery at it's finest... a game where debt management and forward thinking is most critical.  

A GeekBuddy informed me that I didn't have a 'real' train game in my collection and suggested Age of Steam (thanks CB), so after much searching through this site I settled on Steam... to start with. 

I adore Steam and so does my family.  =D  Lucky me!  US/German area maps.  A well balanced and competitive game with a tight enough board map to keep all  players comfortably integrated.  The only randomness in this game is in the initial setup and placement of Goods cubes to cities.  After that I feel that any progress or lack of is of my own choosing.  The 'base rule' Turn Order established by prior selected Action tile is simply brilliant!

I now own and have played AoS and find both the auction for Play Order and Goods Growth/Production mechanism time consuming (though I do, however, understand and appreciate the inherent value of dollar auctions in a network building type game such as this).  I simply prefer the streamlined design and approach to Steam.  

AoS/Steam expansion maps owned to date (for 1-3 players):
Steam Barons
Scotland
Barbados/St Lucia
Jamaica/Puerto Rico
Georgia/South Carolina
Alabama/Louisiana
Mississippi/Golden Spike
America/Europe

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
There's definitely two schools of criticism on this game, those who rate the game as simply an inferior expansion map (with special rules) of the original Age of Steam and those who think it makes necessary improvements to a good, but flawed design. Although I have great love for Age of Steam, thinking of Steam as an expansion doesn't do the game justice.

While I like everything that Steam does, I still think Age of Steam does similar things more interestingly. At the core of why I prefer Age of Steam is the tightness of money. I found the relatively easy flow of money in Steam takes an edge off of everything else that happens in the game. In Age of Steam I'm sweating bullets over every share I issue. In Steam, I took $10, $15 on almost every turn with hardly a concern because of the delivery options available to me to make up the loss. Speaking of delivery options, while I like the idea of having all cubes that will be placed on the board through production known at the outset, I almost wish the cube lots were 2 cubes and not 3. Three seems like a lot and I never once felt like I was searching for deliveries to make. The boards that come with the base game are dense with towns and cities and few longer tracks (4+ tiles) ever need to be built. The choice between taking income or VP's was not at all a difficult decision, as some say it is. It's pretty obvious when to make the switch. The harder decisions were for players who were behind and didn't want to get left in the dust point-wise, but still needed to jack their income. For players in the lead, though, it was hardly a decision at all. I didn't dislike it, but I didn't think was an improvement. Both Age of Steam and Steam are very "gamey" in the calculation of points. But I like gamey!

All of that said, I do think Steam is really good. I'd be happy to play it any time. I just think I'd be happier playing Age of Steam. Steam doesn't improve the game, it just makes it more appealing for those who don't enjoy the harsh economic world of the earlier version. And playing Steam made me finally raise my AoS rating to a 10. I've decided to rate it as a system rather than a single boxed game. As a system it has proven its strength, versatility, and lasting appeal.

RATING: 9
I only played Age of Steam twice before I played this in the expert version. So I'm not biased towards AoS. After first play I can't say which one I prefer and wouldn't mind playing either. The overall feel of the game is very similar with Steam feeling more polished (in a good way). The usual rough edges of Wallace's self-developed games have been flattened, e.g. the costs for building tracks are far more intuitive now and there is no single underpowered action anymore as has been the case in AoS with production. Steam also is more forgiving which is a plus for new gamers but which is probably the biggest drawback for longtime AoS'ers. We never had the feeling of fighting an uphill battle in our game and I can't imagine anybody ever going bankrupt in Steam. Both are great games and for the time being I rate them the same. Much more play needed - and wanted!
---
The Germany map might be too loose for 4 players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Great components, with a couple of minor exceptions (the included
money is pretty bad). Get the first printing while it's still
available for sensible player colors in addition to the ones they
originally intended to have.

I mostly like the rules cleanups from AoS, but there's one change
included that just completely destroys the game: the handling of
income doesn't work at all. The VP vs. Income split doesn't seem to
add much. The insanely low maximum income limit means that everyone
has to take lots of loans to do anything. And the combination of no
income reduction and no ceiling on number of loans means that there's
pretty much no downside to taking the loans. Free money for everyone!
This seems to remove most of the tension from the game.

The lack of income reduction and the loose loan system also means that
there's a much bigger "rich get richer" effect than in AoS. It's just
easier to get a feedback cycle of good board position -> more
money -> better special powers from auction -> even better board
position going. The basic no-auction game kind of fixes this by removing
the auction, and thus breaking the feedback cycle. But I don't think
that just the track building and shipping is interesting enough for
such a long game.

The new production / urbanization actions work fine IMO, and I don't
mind the simplified track building costs.

I can't imagine this getting any more play than a generic mediocre AoS
expansion map though.

RATING: 6
My first play wasn't all that much fun.  We jumped to the advanced game, and all the diehard AoS players grumbled about every change.  I went into a bit of a death spiral and merely held out from going bankrupt for the last half of the game.

My second play was with a smaller group of non-diehards.  We played the introductory game, and I had much more fun.  The introductory game is good, and I liked how the auction was retired.  

The easing on the credit is better - less waiting on players who plot their entire turn before issuing bonds.  The goods growth is more deterministic, and the urbanization is also nice where you pick your set of cubes.

All in all, I like the changes, and may flip my preference over to Steam, which appears to be a very good streamlining to the original.  I like that the new game will easily fit into an evening.

RATING: 8
I really enjoy Age of Steam (and own multiple boards of it).  I do like some aspects or key changes made with this version.  

(1) I like the new Goods Display and how delivery cubes come on to the board along with the new action of Goods Growth.

(2) I like all the new Actions and the money costs associated to take some of those actions.  

However, there is NO taxation in Steam, like there was in Age of Steam.  That's one less small layer that was great, serving as a balancing mechanic to prevent runaway leader issues.  There is also no limit on issuing shares for capitalization as long as you keep growing.  And, finally, scoring has changed from 3 VPs per Income (with -3 per Share Issued) to just 2 VPs.  This makes it more of a track building game.  Bottom line:  Steam is more streamlined but AoS is the real deal.  The best, in my opinion, is the incorporate my above (1) and (2) points into Age of Steam.

RATING: 10
3-5p

2p ok certaines cartes sont mieux adaptées

variante 2p:

Phase 1 - This remains unchanged

Phase 2 - First player pays FULL cost of their bid, the second player pays HALF thie bid rounded up.
So if you bid anything above zero you will always pay something.

Phase 3 - This is the biggest portion of the variant. Action Tiles are set into groupings along the lines of the board.

Group 1 - Turn Order and First Move
Group 2 - Engineer and First Build
Group 3 - City Growth, Locomotive and Urbanization.
If a player chooses a special action from a group, the other player cannot choose ANY OF THE OTHER ACTIONS in that group.

Phase 4 and Phase 5 proceed as normal as well as Phase 6.

A short game is 10 turns, a LONG game is 12 turns.

RATING: 9
I'm now pretty convinced that the genre "train games" is no more meaningful semantically than "network building games," and that's a good thing. Otherwise the world would not have such an array of interesting network building/train games like Chicago Express and Steam (and even Ticket to Ride, which I only enjoy mildly, but respect immensely as a design).

This thought came to mind because playing Steam brought into focus the fact that Chicago Express really is a stock game with a train element as a valuation engine, while Steam is a true train game, where the network building is at the heart of the action. One could reasonably argue that shipping goods is at the heart of Steam, but to me it felt more like a method of providing direction for the track-laying. Regardless, the game offers up some meaty decisions and the most interesting ones revolve around laying track. Even choosing your action tends to revolve around what you want to do with your track over the next couple of rounds. While it is hard to get truly blocked out (and yet it seems to happen at the very worst time), it can be mighty pricey to get to key locations if you don't arrive early enough. You generally need a steady supply of new cubes to ship to keep your head above water, too, so delaying more than a turn before tapping into new cubes can be painful.

This focus on track building makes sense of the cottage industry that has grown up around alternate maps. If you played a single map too many times in a row, you would probably exhaust the questions it asks of the players in a hurry; 4-6 plays, tops. New maps, if well-designed, pose new questions without the heavy overhead of learning an entirely new rules system each time. I suspect playing new maps can help you look through old maps with fresh insight, too.

The one weak spot in Steam is that the economic element doesn't sing, and that stands out in the midst of the interesting decisions elsewhere. While it is possible to get in over your head, it's unlikely, so debt doesn't have much sting in its tail. It's pretty much an automatic decision to let yourself slide 4 or even 5 notches into debt early in the game, since you will defang the debt with a cube delivery or two before you have to pay for it. I'm not sure what I'd like better – the simplicity of this system is almost a necessity to avoid distracting from the heart of the game too much – just that it needs more teeth.

By the way, Steam's set up is much more straightforward than all of the tiles make it look. It's not feasible to sort the track tiles, so you can pour them out in a big pile. If you keep the player tokens separated by colour when you store them, setup shouldn't take more than a couple of minutes.

I'm definitely looking forward to playing more Steam. I'm especially curious to see what effect loosening or tightening the board by playing with three or five does, since my one play is with four (on the Germany map).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
An economic, route-building, pick-up-and-deliver game system.  It requires a lot of planning ahead.  The separated income and VP tracks pose some critical decisions.  What is the optimal time to transition from an economic engine to a VP-collecting engine?  The answer to this question can be highly dependent on the map being played, player position, the goods available, and play-styles of your opponents.  With all the fan-made expansion maps available, this system never gets old.  I like the flexibility in player count, with excellent maps available from 2 to 6 players.

The turn-order [b]auction[/b] (Steam "Standard" rules) makes money very tight, but still more forgiving than Age of Steam because bankruptcy (e.g. [i]player elimination[/i]) is a more difficult condition to trigger.  I count this an improvement, a feature, over AoS.  Paying locomotive costs each turn also means that every game won't be a race to Locomotive level 6.  Played by experienced players, it should theoretically result in the correct [i]pricing[/i] of actions, which should be very dynamic as the game progresses, as it is a function of board position, available cubes, and relative income/VP levels.  This is pretty brutal to new players because it requires careful planning.

Determining turn-order based on previous action-tile selection, the "Base" rules, is a totally different but still wonderful way to play.  This is a better method to learn the game, and is more convenient for online or PBF.  Also one does not need to pay maintenance on one's Locomotive level, so it usually behooves the players to race to level 6 as quickly as possible.

Tense competition for network-building and shipping goods, coupled with excruciating action-selection decisions [i]or[/i] auctions, make Steam one of my favorite euro games for 3-5 players.  Not to mention top-quality game components and a rich theme.  Has all the ingredients to make Steam a seriously wonderful...and wonderfully serious...strategy game.

Update:  I'm open to playing this house-ruling the AoS bankruptcy rules to Steam when playing with experienced players, to [i]raise the stakes[/i].  In other words, if at the end of a round a player is unable to meet one's Locomotive and/or Income expenses, then this opponent is immediately declared bankrupt and out of the game.  But I think this cut-throat rule is best for 2-player matches so that the game ends at this point.

With Steam Expansion Maps #1, #2, #3, AoS 1867 GA Reconstruction, Sicily, Scotland, and AoS Mississippi Riverboats/Goldenspike expansions

RATING: 10

RATING: 4
Okay, reality check.  I had this rated as a 6 as of Jan 2012 and it is now Jan 2015.  I have played Steam a couple of times since then and it has not endeared itself to me.  I realize that many people find the differences between Steam and Age of Steam to be minor, trivial, or mostly inconsequential, and that is fine for them.  If they get the same experience out of both then more power to them.

I, however, have become increasingly disappointed by the major changes that Steam implemented.  

Choosing income vs points seems to be a false choice for most of the game.  There is no incentive to get points early and not a lot of incentive to get income late.  I do not feel like the distinction provides much value.

Goods growth/new cities greatly damage the forward planning in the game.  In Age of Steam, you know what will likely come out and exactly where it will come out.  In Steam you are at the mercy of the other players and while you know what could come out, you, for the most part, have no control over where it will come out, nor when it will come out.  This, for me, generates more chaos than the random goods growth of Age of Steam.  You should keep in mind that I dislike randomness and luck in games.

As of Jan 2015 I am dropping this down to a 4.

Basic Game
Interesting.  The basic game is much better than RRT, but not as good as AoS.  Not having to plan expenses/shares takes a lot of punch out of the game.

While not having an auction tended to speed things up, the additional urbanization and production options and decisions tended to slow it back down in my group.  I look forward to the Standard Game.

1 play, 4 players, US map
2 plays, 5 players, Europe map

Standard Game
I finally got around to playing the standard game.  I probably won't play it that way again, as long as Age of Steam is available.  AoS is rougher around the edges, but I *like* it that way.  

1 play, 3 players, Europe map
1 play, 3 players, Brussels Metro map

Steam Barons

1 play, 3 players, England map

RATING: 9
Rapidly becoming one of my favourite "long" games.  Theme is brilliantly implemented.  The lack of luck is a plus as players strive to build the best railways while being mindful of opponents strategies to do the same.  It's one of those games that will get fiendishly competitive once people pick up the basic strategies making for a compelling and satisfying gaming session.

Having to decide how much money you want before each round just adds to analysis paralysis and the auctions mean that you often don't have enough cash to compete with other players who can pull away while others are contending with bankruptcy issues.  With the base version money is always available meaning all players can always compete which is more satisfying.

RATING: 7
Tenuous - likely to be removed due to me getting AoS

It feels weird having this instead of Age of Steam which I'm quite sure I'd enjoy even more - but this is the one I'll get to play hereabouts so its both or this (and this for now then).

Based on a single digital play, I like it! It's far more abstracted and gamey than I thought it would be, but I can see why its necessary. At its core it really is combative network building, where you need to position yourself right both spatially and in the crucial auctions - With some tougher opposition I can easily see how this game would become quite the analytical grinder, as doing sums in your head and planning ahead is crucial. Your network and that of other players immediately are tightly interwoven and you need to see where they can get "at you". It was surprisingly confrontational, and the whole using other player's links to deliver adds to that (and while I can see the kingmaker scenario it doesn't bother me much, but it requires players to not "throw the game" by paying attention but they ought to do that anyway so)!

I also see (in part) how Steam differs from Age of Steam. And it affects the game in what I can only see to be a negative direction where the income / vp split just feels silly and how the goods growth is done ruins a lot of the spatial lookahead. Still I found I like this quite a bit (to the extent where I think I'd be happy to play both), but I would like to test the waters with those I tend to play with and see if they feel the same, otherwise I'm just ending up with an unplayed game.

Also, markedly less opaque than I had assumed it would be but sometimes that's what you want.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Game in a Nutshell:  Borrow money, lay track, and deliver goods to earn income or gain victory points.

Played as: 3,4 player

Pros:  A game where pretty much everything is contested.  Whether it’s a special action, claiming the prime routes, or who’ll deliver those ever diminishing cubes first, you’re going to need to carefully plan for it in an ever fluid player-dictated environment.   Central to everything is how you start the game with nothing and need to go in the hole to fund your start up.  Climbing out of that hole is the best part as prices can escalate, especially in the auction, which can sometimes make or break your plans for the rest of the round. To sum it up the game is chock full of interesting decisions and situations.

Cons:  It’s no wonder there are so many expansions for the "Steam" games since it doesn’t take long for play to feel scripted on any given map. The included U.S. map really seems to channel play with three players.  Lacks a good turn-structure player aid as there is already enough to think about than remembering which of seven phases is next in the round.  It also doesn’t help the rule book is written like a magazine article rather than something outlined for easy digestion and quick reference.

Other:  The "Basic" rules didn’t do much for me. The economic crunchy-ness was lacking and it simply became an exercise in who got to do what first. My advice is skip the basic game and go straight for the standard rules which are actually easier for new players to learn anyway.  Let the newbies buy capital as needed (instead of at the start of the round) for maybe the first game, and that’s it. Still not gentle enough? Then perhaps Steam wasn’t the right fit. 

Best Use:  A heavier Euro for a lighter crowd.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[microbadge=1714]

[u]08-Feb-2013[/u]:  This is a fairly straightforward train game (at least using the standard rules).  Everyone chooses a role/action at the beginning of the turn which they can use for additional actions during their standard turn of placing track and delivering goods.  The roles (again, under the standard ruleset) determine the next round's player order.  Based on how many goods and how far you deliver them on your turn will add to your income or your final VP.

The base game comes with two maps depending on player count, and there are many, many additional maps available for purchase.  The way income works can be a little wonky at first, but after a play or two, you should be creating a solid rail system and fighting over who will deliver those goods the furthest.

The components are excellent - nice, thick board; lots of pretty wooden cubes, sturdy tiles.  I can't wait to delve further into this one...hopefully soon.

[u]26-Jan-2014[/u]:  So I've played this several more times, all solo except for one PBF game (which was a lot of fun!).  I've played multiplayer solo, using a solo variant, and with a solo specific map.  I really do enjoy this game and am hoping that the new SoloPlay rules will allow me to get more out of this game than I probably will otherwise.

I still think it's an excellent game.  I love the map and watching the network of rails build and expand throughout the game.  I like how all of the markers look at the end of the game across the board.  I'm only familar with the Basic rules, and I think they work quite well with their ranking for next turn order.

This style of game does appeal to me and I may even look to add another train game or two over time.

RATING: 6
This game has a brutal learning curve, and it is utterly unforgiving of mistakes. Add to that the fact that it is an exercise in brainblistering analysis paralysis and you have a game that takes too long, and is thus absent the significant tension caused by a tight flow of action and choice. I am irritated by this, perhaps more than I should be, but I felt like, in comparison to other economic games (Container, Imperial, CE) where the possibilities inherent in the mechanics are explicit and apparent, Steam has too much need for repeat play to grok it. That said, I was definitely intrigued by it, and I will play it again, but the experience, both intellectually and emotionally was more of a grind than I like. Perhaps future plays will soften the harsh edges?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Yet another version of Age of Steam, with a "base" version and a "standard" version. The base game, is easier to play, and is summarized here: 

1. There is a VP track and an income track (ranging from -10 to +10). When one ships goods, they can choose to increase either VPs or income; one or the other but not both. At the end of the turn, if one has positive income, they get that amount, if they are in the negative, they have to pay that amount. Instead of issuing shares, if one needs money, they simply lower their income by one, and get $5. At the end of the game, VPs for income are counted as follows: if you are positive, that amount is halved and added to your score. If you have negative income, that amount is multiplied by two and subtracted from your score. 

2. You only bid for starting position at the beginning of the game. Players bid to see who goes first, then players bid next to see who goes second and so on. The player winning the bid then selects a tile from the following: #1 Go first in the next round; #2 Ship cubes first, #3 Engineer: build 4 track; #4 build first, #5 City growth, adding three cubes to a city, #6 Locomotive, adding a locomotive to your train at a cost of $4 plus the level you are upgrading to, #7 Urbanization, costs $6, lets you add a new city with three colored cubes. On the next turn, turn order is determined by the number of your tile. Whoever has the lowest tile number, gets to go first. 

3. The gameboard also has 12 spots for three randomly drawn colored cubes. Whoever chooses urbanization or city growth gets to choose a batch of cubes to put on a city. When all the batches of cubes run out, there is no replenishment. A city may only get at most one added batch of cubes. A token is used to mark cities that have received a batch of cubes.
 
There are two maps, a USA/Canada and Germany map.

With 4 players, the game seems balanced and tense as the original AoS. With 5 players, near the end, we were running out of straight and curved tracks, so we could not complete some links at the end, which skewed the game a bit. I would rate this game a 7, but in the five player game, with tracks running out, it doesn't really help the game, so I would give it a rating of 6.5. I think they could have solved this problem by supplying a few more straight and curved tracks. I enjoyed this game with 3 players, and the turns go fast. So I think the sweet spot is 3-4 players. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8

edit: even better with more plays, and more players. I'm really happy to own this.

It is a bit... I'm not sure, mechanical? emotionless? Building, planning, calculating. A cube here, bits of track there. The game pushes you towards doing the best you can, and hoping it will be enough. Tense, cerebral.

I do miss moments where you can relax, sit back, feel confident that you're doing well, seeking opportunities for clever tactical moves, swingy plays, bluff, bribe, negotiate... Steam has none of that. It's a struggle against the game itself, turn after turn after turn. Multiplayer chess.
